It is a decision that did not come easily, nor was it made quickly.\"\n\n\"Racing is a labor-intensive, humbling sport. It requires unwavering commitment and vast resources, with a 365-day mindset to be better than everyone else. It's part of what makes success so rewarding.\"\n\n\"But the commitment needed to extract maximum performance while providing sustainability is incredibly demanding, and we've reached a point in our respective personal and business lives where it's time to pass the torch.\"\n\n\"We're proud of all the wins and championships we've earned since joining together in 2009, but even more special is the culture we built and the friendships we forged as we committed to a common cause - winning races and collecting trophies.\"\n\n\"That is the same commitment we made to our personnel, our partners and our fans coming into this year, and that commitment will remain through the season finale at Phoenix.\"\n\n\"We have tremendous respect and appreciation for all of our employees, and we will work {"uri": "8150485696", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "20:45:56", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T20:45:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T20:43:44Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.615686297416687, "url": "https://www.nytimes.com/athletic/4108348/2023/01/20/kevin-harvick-replacement-stewart-haas/", "title": "Who'll replace Kevin Harvick at Stewart-Haas Racing? Here are the top options", "body": "Only a select number of NASCAR organizations can provide a driver the realistic expectation of contending for race wins, playoff berths and championship-round appearances on a yearly basis. Should such an opportunity -- a potentially career-altering one -- open up, that ride becomes coveted because of the possibilities it offers.\n\nStewart-Haas Racing is one of those elite organizations. The team co-owned by Tony Stewart and Gene Haas has won two championships over the past dozen years, the same number as Joe Gibbs Racing and trailing only Hendrick Motorsports (four) and Team Penske (three) during this span. And now, with Kevin Harvick announcing last week that he's retiring after the 2023 season, SHR's No. 4 car has a vacancy that needs filling.\n\nYet unlike most occasions when a team the caliber of SHR has an opening, the candidate pool to replace Harvick doesn't appear to be as deep as it normally would. Over the past year, Kyle Larson, William Byron, Christopher Bell, Erik Jones, Ryan Blaney and Joey Logano all signed multi-year contract extensions with their respective teams, while Kyle Busch and Tyler Reddick joined new teams under multi-year deals.\n\nEach of the aforementioned eight drivers would've been a worthy successor to Harvick, giving SHR a driver possessing the talent to consistently challenge for wins and championships. And had Harvick announced last January that 2022 would be his swan song, it stands to reason that SHR would've had an interest in signing one of them to drive the No. 4. Alas, SHR is now looking at a whittled-down list of options and the distinct possibility it won't be able to land an A-tier replacement.\n\nSo then, based on the current landscape, what direction does SHR go? The Athletic spoke to multiple people across the NASCAR industry to gauge who should be considered a realistic candidate, and while the list of potential replacements looks much different than it would've a year ago, the consensus is that solid options still exist.\n\nThe most cited name was Ross Chastain. The star driver is entering the final year of his contract with Trackhouse Racing. He was described by one person as a \"home run hire that would give SHR little drop-off on the performance side.\" Helping his candidacy is that he has a personality that appeals to sponsors -- a potentially key factor as it's unknown whether Harvick's longtime primary sponsor, Anheuser-Busch, will continue its support or also depart.\n\nChastain demonstrated in 2022 what he's capable of when he has a team around him, winning two races and finishing runner-up in the championship. But the question is whether SHR represents a enough of an upgrade over Trackhouse to entice the loyal Chastain to leave the team that provided him a big break. A strong bond exists between Chastain and team owner Justin Marks, making it hard to envision Chastain foregoing that relationship for an unknown entity where he may not fit as well.\n\nThe other most cited name was Jones, even though he's in the first year of a multi-year contract with the rebranded Legacy Motor Club. As his pair of Southern 500 wins can attest, Jones' talent is unmistakable. At age 26, he brings with him the capability to be a mainstay that SHR can build around for the next decade-plus.\n\nThat Jones is under contract to Legacy presents a hurdle, though not insurmountable. Contract buyouts can and do happen -- like last season when 23XI Racing untangled Reddick's final year of his contract with Richard Childress Racing.\n\nOf course, both SHR and Jones would have to want this, and there's no certainty on this front. SHR has had the opportunity to sign Jones twice previously and passed both times, so what would be different about this go-round? And would Jones, who's established a home at Legacy where he's viewed as the main guy, want to again experience life at a four-car behemoth after a rough ending at the similarly sized JGR?\n\nBeyond Chastain and Jones, the alternatives become a lot less clear-cut.\n\nAlex Bowman and Daniel Su\u00e1rez are on expiring contracts with their respective teams, Hendrick and Trackhouse. But leaving their situation seems unlikely. Hendrick and sponsor Ally are happy with Bowman, while Su\u00e1rez, who drove for SHR in 2019, is quite settled at Trackhouse. Although things can always change, a whole lot would have to unexpectedly shift to see either jumping.\n\nStaying in-house, SHR could always tab Cole Custer or Riley Herbst, both of whom drive for the team's Xfinity Series program. Promoting either, however, would elicit more of a collective shrug reaction than a \"wow\" that the team should want in naming Harvick's successor. Custer largely underperformed in three Cup seasons and needs a monster season in Xfinity to reestablish himself. Herbst is winless in 109 career Xfinity starts and his spot on SHR's roster has more to do with the funding he brings than his results.\n\nAnother name to watch from the lower divisions is reigning Truck Series champion Zane Smith. He's part of the Ford program, and that association cannot be ignored as Ford-supported SHR goes through this process. The 23-year-old is regarded as a top prospect, earning praise for a smooth style that doesn't lend itself to over-driving -- a characteristic you don't often see from young drivers.\n\nIf SHR is unable to secure a long-term solution, a stopgap for a year or two could be in play. A name often identified under this scenario is Michael McDowell. The veteran has done a lot with a little at Front Row Motorsports, including a Daytona 500 win in 2021 and last year setting highs in top-10 finishes, laps led, average starting position and average finishing position. He'd likely acquit himself well at a powerhouse team, and SHR could do a lot worse than having McDowell serve as a bridge.\n\nAnd there is the very real possibility that SHR elects to go with a candidate not on this board, someone unforeseen. After all, what were the odds last year on Kyle Busch not re-signing with JGR to join the team owned by someone who once punched him? Yet that unlikelihood is now a reality.\n\nWhatever direction Stewart and Haas go, who's behind the wheel of the SHR No. 4 car in 2024 is guaranteed to be a dominant storyline until a decision is {"uri": "8150554325", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "21:58:42", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T21:58:42Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T21:57:57Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.6117647290229797, "url": "https://www.nytimes.com/athletic/5524911/2024/05/28/stewart-haas-racing-nascar-shut-down-leadership/", "title": "Bianchi: Stewart-Haas Racing's NASCAR demise begins and ends with leadership", "body": "When Tony Stewart and Gene Haas shared a stage in July 2008 to announce the formation of Stewart-Haas Racing, it was clear this had the makings of NASCAR's next dynasty.\n\nStewart was an elite driver with two Cup Series championships whose star power would attract key sponsors and personnel to propel SHR upward. Stewart's hard-edged, no-excuses-tolerated mentality would become the underlying characteristic that defined SHR. The billionaire Haas was the money man willing to spend what it took to bring his then-struggling Haas CNC Racing team the success it never found.\n\nAlmost instantly, Stewart and Haas delivered on their promises, with a fast rise to powerhouse status. Stewart won four times in his first year as co-owner, then won the 2011 championship two years later. The team had an array of high-profile sponsors and continued to make key free-agent signings, including landing Kevin Harvick, who then won the team its second title in 2014.\n\nJust as Stewart and Haas said the team would, SHR had shoved its way into NASCAR's penthouse alongside elite organizations such as Hendrick Motorsports, Joe Gibbs Racing and Team Penske. And similar to Hendrick, JGR and Penske, all of which have been NASCAR teams for 30-plus years, SHR seemingly had planted firm roots that ensured it would continue for decades.\n\nExcept SHR is no Hendrick, JGR or Penske. The SHR dynasty is not only over, its days as an operational Cup team have a definitive end date. On Tuesday, Stewart and Haas announced their team will shut down at the end of the year.\n\nNASCAR is better with teams like SHR, those committed to winning, on its grid. And SHR has done plenty of winning since first hitting the track in 2009; only Hendrick (six titles) and Penske (four) have amassed more championships during this span.\n\nBut the key tenet of most long-standing teams is strong leadership. It's why Hendrick, JGR and Penske have experienced lasting success on the track. At SHR, though, a lack of leadership is why SHR is no longer viewed as a powerhouse within the garage. And why it will soon cease to exist.\n\nDespite their public stance that they are very involved, Stewart and Haas have effectively become absentee owners in recent years. They aren't regularly at the track. Each has other racing ventures where they'd rather spend their time. Stewart is both an owner and driver in NHRA. Haas owns a Formula One team.\n\nIt is their prerogative to choose where to spend their time, but while Stewart and Haas have been focused elsewhere, their NASCAR dynasty has crumbled. SHR has become a facsimile of the organization it once was. This despite having an array of talented drivers, crew chiefs, engineers and others behind the scenes, all of whom have been given little support in recent years but still grind out solid results.\n\nA telling glimpse as to why SHR has suffered a rapid downward spiral: Two weeks ago, Harvick, who retired after the 2023 season after 10 years with SHR, returned in a one-off at the All-Star Race to practice and qualify Kyle Larson's Hendrick-owned car. With this being Harvick's first opportunity to work closely with a Hendrick team and see how they operate, the 2014 Cup champion was asked to compare and contrast the two organizations.\n\n\"I heard from the owner twice in two weeks, so that's different,\" Harvick said. \"It's interesting to see just the race shop, the structure and the way that everybody goes about it differently.\n\n\"There's a million different ways you can do things. The thing that sticks out for me about Hendrick Motorsports in general, it's truly run like a business that is part of an actual structure of how things flow and who you talk to. There's just the depth of the business side and the racing side, it's deep. That's pretty eye-opening -- just the structure from the whole thing. And I like structure. That's something that has been good to see. ...\n\n\"(At SHR), I had a very special group of people that succeeded in a structure that wasn't as structured in what we're in in this situation (at Hendrick). I had a lot of success at Stewart-Haas Racing, and all the things we did I'm super proud of and love the relationships that we have, but it's just different, vastly different cultures.\"\n\nHarvick's words speak volumes as to why SHR did not fulfill its potential. Leadership, culture and structure matter significantly. It is why great teams remain great.\n\nSuch guiding principles define organizations like Hendrick. They once defined SHR too. But all this is in the past. Not the present, and definitely not the future.\n\nThis was made known on Tuesday.", "source": {"uri": "nytimes.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The New York Times"}, "authors": [{"uri": It requires unwavering commitment and vast resources, with a 365-day mindset to be better than everyone else. It's part of what makes success so rewarding. But the commitment needed to extract maximum performance while providing sustainability is incredibly demanding, and we've reached a point in our respective personal and business lives where it's time to pass the torch.\"\n\nSHR this year fields Cup cars for Josh Berry, Chase Briscoe, Noah Gragson and Ryan Preece. {"uri": "8150485694", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "20:45:39", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T20:45:39Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T20:43:44Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5529412031173706, "url": "https://www.nytimes.com/athletic/4630385/2023/06/21/josh-berry-stewart-haas-racing-kevin-harvick/", "title": "Why Josh Berry was Stewart-Haas Racing's choice to replace Kevin Harvick", "body": "CONCORD, N.C. -- It's rare that a 32-year-old driver with minimal Cup Series experience lands a full-time ride with a prominent NASCAR team. And if such an opportunity does materialize, it often involves the driver bringing considerable funding with them to underwrite the chance even if their talent merits it.\n\nJosh Berry is the exception.\n\nNext year, Berry, who will turn 33 in October, will be in Cup where he'll drive Stewart-Haas Racing's No. 4 car, replacing the retired Kevin Harvick. It's a plum opportunity with a top organization, the kind of job a driver dreams about securing.\n\nAnd that SHR, co-owned by Gene Haas and Tony Stewart, tabbed Berry to fill Harvick's spot is a bit improbable. Such opportunities aren't often afforded to drivers of Berry's ilk -- though he is certainly deserving.\n\n\"I'm not interested in some kid's father coming in and buying their way into the Cup Series. I have zero interest in that,\" Stewart said. \"We want guys who earn their way, work hard, understand the values it takes to be a top-tier driver -- not one who just got his high school diploma and now all of a sudden he's a Cup driver.\n\n\"Josh has put the time and effort in. Every time he's got an opportunity, he's made the most of it, and that's the traits that championship drivers are built off of, not the easy ticket to get there.\"\n\nIf Stewart sought a driver who scraped, fought and persevered to finally get a coveted shot at the big time with a big-time team, then Berry certainly fits that description.\n\nA Hendersonville, Tenn., native, Berry was racing Legend cars at Nashville Fairgrounds Speedway in 2008 when on a whim he decided to reach out to Dale Earnhardt Jr. for advice. A friendship was born and a few years later Berry, who worked full-time as a bank teller to supplement his income, reached out to Earnhardt when he found himself at a career crossroads. He wanted to keep racing, progressing up the developmental ladder, except he lacked funding.\n\nImpressed by Berry's ability and determination, Earnhardt offered Berry the chance in 2010 to drive for his Late Model team that competed across the Mid-Atlantic region. It required that Berry relocate to the Charlotte area, and since Berry knew few people and had nowhere to live, he moved in with Earnhardt's mom. Before too long, he had become a member of the family.\n\nMaking the most of the opportunity, Berry won numerous races and multiple championships in various divisions. Occasional starts with the Xfinity Series team Earnhardt co-owned eventually came Berry's way, and more often than not he delivered impressive results, including a seventh at Richmond in Sept. 2015.\n\nFor sure sponsors would come calling, Earnhardt thought, and Berry would soon be in Xfinity on a permanent basis.\n\nNot quite. A lack of sponsorship continued to be a foreboding roadblock.\n\nDespite possessing talent and even with Earnhardt's unwavering support, a full-time opportunity didn't come Berry's way until 2022. In the meantime, he continued to make the most of his chances, winning his first Xfinity race in 2021 and another later that season. That first one came at Martinsville, which Earnhardt admitted made him cry \"like if he was my brother. It's hard to describe the emotion, but it was incredible.\"\n\nOn the Cup side, Berry earned a spot filling in this spring at Hendrick Motorsports when two of its Cup drivers, Chase Elliott and Alex Bowman, missed time due to injury. Further demonstrating he was ready for Cup, Berry dazzled, including a second-place finish at Richmond.\n\n\"The best evidence is exactly what he did in (filling in for Elliott and Bowman),\" Harvick said. \"I mean, jumping into those cars when the seats don't fit, he doesn't know the people very well and being competitive and figuring it out.\"\n\nBut while NASCAR starts may have been infrequent, folks were watching. Harvick's championship-winning crew chief, Rodney Childers, himself a former driver in many of the grassroots series Berry competed in, was paying attention. So too were other notable decision-makers within the organization. When SHR chief competition officer Greg Zipadelli began doing research on Berry, he quickly surmised that he was an ideal fit.\n\n\"His want and willingness to work,\" Zipadelli said regarding what traits stood out. \"... We felt like he was mentally strong, was able to deal with different sorts of circumstances, and he's been good at a lot of different racetracks.\"\n\nSometime over the offseason, an SHR executive walked into Childers' office with a quasi-mandate. Harvick, the 2014 Cup champion, was retiring after the 2023 season and SHR needed to start putting together a list of potential replacements. The organization wanted Childers' feedback, making it clear his opinion carried significant weight.\n\nThe crew chief went home and thoroughly researched who was available, who'd be a good fit and who could handle the pressure of filling the shoes of a driver considered one of NASCAR's all-time best. One name immediately stood out. Excited by the chance to work with Berry, Childers elected to stay on as crew chief of the No. 4 team instead of opting for a behind-the-scenes role with SHR that got him off the road.\n\n\"I think everybody knows that I'm a Josh Berry fan,\" Childers said. \"I told the shop guys at Stewart-Haas this morning that I wish they could all go back through (Berry's) phone and count how many times that I text him saying if that guy could ever get the right opportunity, he's going to show something.\"\n\nSimilar to Childers, the triumvirate of Stewart, Harvick and Zipadelli had Berry's name atop their lists. It didn't matter to any of them that Berry was in his 30s, well past an age when most talented drivers have already advanced to Cup.\n\nIn fact, that Berry was older worked to his advantage.\n\n\"Josh is sort of a good example of how maybe it might turn back the other direction,\" Earnhardt said. \"You don't have to be 18 to 20 years old rushing through the Xfinity Series. That there's guys that take their time to develop in the Truck and Xfinity Series, get out of there at 24, 25, 26 years old and start driving cars at 27, 28 years old and be great.\"\n\nSaid Harvick: \"He's been dragged through the mud and had good times and bad times and figured out how to make it to the next event and be successful.\"\n\nBerry represents an anomaly. Drivers his age aren't supposed to be handpicked to replace a certain Hall of Famer in Harvick.\n\nYet there he was Wednesday afternoon sharing a dais with Stewart, Harvick, Childers and Zipadelli. The four longtime SHR employees spoke glowingly about the team's newest hire and how he had earned this chance.\n\n\"It's just cool as crap today to see him up here with us, to be able to go racing with him and to have that same common goal,\" Childers said.\n\n\"Turnkey\" was a word mentioned a few times on Wednesday. Because of his age, Berry possesses a level of maturity often absent in most young drivers moving to Cup, something that should work to his advantage next year when he will in all likelihood be the oldest rookie on the grid.\n\n\"It's important to have seasoned, experienced, mature people because Cup racing, especially when you're just starting, can eat you up,\" Harvick said. \"And it usually eats you up because of the pressure or the questions and all the stuff outside of the car, and then it starts to affect what you do inside of the car. So having somebody that can deal with that in a mature way right off the bat is important.\"\n\nPerhaps if Berry does well, Earnhardt said, other teams may be inclined to give older drivers a chance. Even if they aren't bringing funding with them.\n\nTime will tell. Just the fact, though, that Berry is in this position serves as inspiration to other grassroots drivers out there who've been told they're too old and/or lack the necessary money.\n\n\"He definitely should be. Absolutely,\" Stewart said. \"This should be an inspiration to drivers that put the work in, put the time in, that if you do the right things, you win enough races, you're going to get enough eyes on you that you'll get an opportunity, eventually.\"\n\nIn many ways, Berry's background is similar to that of Stewart's or Harvick's. And it's why he is unquestionably the logical choice to drive the No. 4 SHR car in 2024.\n\n\"There's a small group of those true racers like Josh that just put their head down,\" Harvick said, \"and it's just, 'Hey, I need to go out and win this week. I got to put food on the table and survive so I'm going to go race here, I'm going to go race there and if I don't do good at my Xfinity opportunity, I'm not going to get a Cup opportunity.' And when you can survive in those life-or-death situations and be successful and take it to the next step and use that as a stepping stone to the next opportunity, those are the guys that you {"uri": "8150485699", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "20:44:07", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T20:44:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T20:43:44Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5098039507865906, "url": "https://www.nytimes.com/athletic/1593623/2020/02/08/two-more-years-kevin-harvicks-statistical-decline-is-inevitable-but-hes-still-valuable-to-stewart-haas-racing/", "title": "Two more years: Kevin Harvick's statistical decline is inevitable, but he's still valuable to Stewart-Haas Racing", "body": "Kevin Harvick confirmed a two-year extension with Stewart-Haas Racing on Saturday, locking him into the organization's No. 4 car through the 2023 season. As marquee drivers and personnel changes are made, The Athletic will offer an analysis on what the move means.\n\nKevin Harvick confirmed Saturday he has signed a two-year contract extension; thereafter, Stewart-Haas Racing's grand designs rapidly crystallized.\n\nHis new agreement takes him through his age-47 season, an age we haven't seen NASCAR Cup drivers regularly attempt to surpass since the turn of the millennium. A glance at the average driver's aging curve suggests drivers are past their statistical peak after age 39 and fall into decline -- sometimes precipitously -- after age 41. He's managed to sustain his prime-age production powers. Such a feat might have convinced him to press forward despite an eventual decline to chase a second championship with a team tailored to his specific talents.\n\nAn initial sign of decline might have already occurred. Harvick's previously reliable passing fell below par in 2019, the first time he turned in a negative surplus passing value since the metric's invention in 2014. It cost him five positions from his expected pass differential (a 156-position swing from his effort the previous year). He led the series in SPV, which measures pass efficiency, in 2016 and ranked second in 2015 and in 2018 (he ranked 13th in 2019). Per a regression analysis, his 2020 season is projected for a Production in Equal Equipment Rating in line with the 3.347 he earned last year, which ranked fourth but represented a 1.500-point drop from a career-best mark in 2018.\n\nBut the new commitment to SHR likely has little to do with statistics. Harvick has long eyed the TV booth but there's no clear seat available and NASCAR's agreement with network partners Fox and NBC doesn't conclude until after the 2024 season. He recently announced he's cutting back on the number of cameo broadcasting appearances for support races to spend time more with his wife and two children.\n\nThe notion of delaying his foray into broadcasting is music to the ears of SHR, a well-built, engineering-centric organization at risk of losing its bellwether driver and the plethora of knowledge he brought to the program. Hendrick Motorsports hasn't had industry-leading speed since Jeff Gordon retired as a full-time driver in 2015, and the idea those two occurrences correlate should be enough to petrify SHR, which lacks the star power and a Hall of Fame-level mind past Harvick. To wit, SHR's No. 4 entry has produced the fastest car in the series in five of the six seasons since Harvick joined the team and ranked second in the outlier in 2017. It'd be ideal to keep hold of a part of the knowledge base responsible for such an understated yet important accomplishment.\n\nHarvick remaining at SHR means his savvy, regardless of whether he's physically able to implement it or execute on it, stays in house to benefit its next generation, which includes rookie Cole Custer, 22.\n\nHis staying put also represents a hedge on SHR's chances with this year's extraordinarily deep free-agent class, which includes Kyle Larson, Brad Keselowski, Ryan Blaney and Erik Jones, whose names are all likely on a whiteboard somewhere deep in an office on the organization's Kannapolis, N.C., campus. If SHR strikes out on all its top choices, Harvick remains in play and crew chief Rodney Childers goes into career extension mode -- and that means an improved focus on positional defense in vulnerable long-run situations -- until another free agent to its liking surfaces. If SHR does land one of the marquee available drivers, Harvick could prove a sound mind during competition meetings, helping with the new hire's integration. The inverse should suit Harvick as well: He's gone on the record about his delight for heightened feedback from fellow SHR drivers.\n\nRegardless, Harvick's extension puts SHR in a good spot: Even with diminished skills but a keen mind, Harvick could still ensure its equipment remains on elite footing well into the era of the Generation 7 car. Eventually, a competitive environment becomes an attractive free-agent destination; SHR's frugality in recent years belies its spending capability, and while no organization has complete control over the landing spots of every potential domino, it can show well, at the very least, and offer fringe benefits. Access to the Harvick notebook and, if he's willing, his tutelage are unique carrots to dangle.\n\nDiminished productivity is all but an inevitability, but a knowledge base within arm's reach is {"uri": "8150571546", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "22:16:43", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T22:16:43Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T22:15:30Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5098039507865906, "url": "https://www.sbnation.com/formula-one/2024/5/28/24166623/f1-nascar-stewart-haas", "title": "What the Stewart-Haas news could mean for Haas F1", "body": "The motorsport world received confirmation on Tuesday that one of the more successful teams in NASCAR would be shutting down following the 2024 season. After weeks of speculation, Stewart-Haas announced on Tuesday in a statement that the team would be closing operations at the end of the year.\n\nWhat might this mean for the Haas Formula 1 team?\n\nFirst, the news out of NASCAR. In a statement co-owners Tony Stewart and Gene Haas announced that at the end of 2024 the team, which was formed back in 2009 and delivered a pair of NASCAR Cup titles -- Stewart's title in 2011 and Kevin Harvick's in 2014 -- would be ceasing operations:\n\n\"We have made the difficult decision to close Stewart-Haas Racing at the conclusion of the 2024 season. It is a decision that did not come easily, nor was it made quickly.\n\n\"Racing is a labor-intensive, humbling sport. It requires unwavering commitment and vast resources, with a 365-day mindset to be better than everyone else. It's part of what makes success so rewarding.\n\n\"But the commitment needed to extract maximum performance while providing sustainability is incredibly demanding, and we've reached a point in our respective personal and business lives where it's time to pass the torch.\n\n\"We're proud of all the wins and championships we've earned since joining together in 2009, but even more special is the culture we built and the friendships we forged as we committed to a common cause -- winning races and collecting trophies.\n\n\"That is the same commitment we made to our personnel, our partners and our fans coming into this year, and that commitment will remain through the season finale at Phoenix.\n\n\"We have tremendous respect and appreciation for all of our employees, and we will work diligently to assist them during this transition to find new opportunities beyond the 2024 race season.\"\n\nStewart-Haas currently has four drivers in the NASCAR Cup series -- Josh Berry, Noah Gragson, Chase Briscoe and Ryan Preece -- as well as Cole Custer and Riley Herbst in NASCAR's Xfinity series.\n\nWith the team's relationship with Ford set to end at the conclusion of the 2024 season, and without a manufacturer lined up for the 2025 campaign, rumors of this move were lingering in the motorsport world for weeks.\n\nNow the question because what impact, if any, could this have on the Haas F1 team?\n\nAt first blush, perhaps no impact at all. Gene Haas has been spending more time with the F1 operation in recent months, and ahead of the 2024 season a leadership change came. Guenther Steiner, the only Team Principal in Haas F1 history, was out, replaced by Ayao Komatsu.\n\nThat move has paid some early dividends, as Haas currently sit seventh in the Constructors' Championship, ahead of their last-place finish a year ago. It also reflects the motive for the change, according to Haas himself. \"It came down to performance,\" Haas told Lawrence Barretto back in January when the move was announced. \"Here we are in our eighth year, over 160 races - we have never had a podium. The last couple of years, we've been 10th or ninth.\n\n\"I'm not sitting here saying it's Guenther's fault, or anything like that, but it just seems like this was an appropriate time to make a change and try a different direction, because it doesn't seem like continuing with what we had is really going to work.\"\n\nDuring that same conversation, Haas reiterated his commitment to the F1 operation.\n\n\"I didn't get into F1 to sell [the team],\" said Haas back in January to F1.com. \"I did it because I wanted to race. Guenther had the same perspective. We're not here to cash out, we want to race and be competitive. If you look at any team, historically, they have had a lot of good years and a lot of bad years.\n\n\"Surviving is one of the characteristics of getting better. As long as you can survive, you always have another year to prove your worthiness. This is a big change. Losing Guenther is going to cause the team to have to focus on other aspects. We will hopefully come out better for it.\"\n\nAnd this answer to Barretto last January certainly looks interesting given the Stewart-Haas news: \"There is a perception we spend a lot less money; we're usually within $10m of the budget limit,\" said Haas. \"I just think we don't do a very good job of spending that money. A lot of teams have had previous investments in their infrastructure, buildings, equipment and personnel. Our model was to outsource a lot of that. We spend a lot of money. We haven't exceeded the cap but we're pretty darn close to it. I just don't think we're doing a very good job of spending it in the most effective way.\n\nThat's one of the reasons we have survived - because we are so conscious of how we spend money,\" added Haas. \"Being efficient at what we do is going to make sure we survive in this series. We're one of the longest surviving teams, everyone else [other new teams] have had the tendency to spend all their money in the first few years and then they go out of business.\n\n\"We survived for eight years, and we're not in a situation where we are going to go out of business. But I certainly want to be able to survive for the next 10 years.\"\n\nSo closing down Stewart-Haas may be another efficient move from the Haas that allows the F1 operation to continue on, and potentially thrive given their relationship with Ferrari.\n\nHowever, this news comes just as discussions around the proposed Andretti-Cadillac team have shifted from that organization becoming the 11th team on the grid, to that team potentially buying an existing team on the grid. Both Red Bull Team Principal Christian Horner, and FIA President Mohammed Ben Sulayem raised that proposition this past weekend during the Monaco Grand Prix.\n\n\"I have no doubt FOM and Liberty [Media -- F1's ownership group] would love to see other teams as long as they are OEMs [Original Equipment/Car Manufacturers],\" said Ben Sulayem in Monaco to Reuters. \"I would advise them [Andretti-Cadillac] to go and buy another team, not to come as the 11th team.\"\n\nAccording to the FIA President, it comes down to a matter of quality, not quantity.\n\n\"I feel that some teams need to be refreshed. What is better? To have 11 teams as a number or 10 and they are strong? I still believe we should have more teams but not any teams. The right teams,\" added Ben Sulayem.\n\n\"It's not about the number, it's about the quality.\"\n\nHorner mirrored that sentiment ahead of the Monaco Grand Prix.\n\n\"I think Andretti is a great racing heritage,\" said Horner to assembled media in Monaco. \"Mario is a legend of the sport and of course, [they are partnered with] Cadillac, the huge automotive manufacturer from the US.\n\n\"I think Formula 1 have said in 2028, if they were to come with their own engine, they would obviously review it but I think in addition to that, I think that if Andretti were to want to come, a little like Audi acquired Sauber, I think to protect the current franchises' stability that we have in the sport, then, obviously, their best route to the goal is to acquire one of the existing teams.\"\n\nPerhaps the Stewart-Haas news has no impact at all on the F1 operation, and maybe even sees the team take another step forward due to the efficiency argument advanced by Haas last January.\n\nOr, perhaps it does have an impact, given the latest twist in the ongoing Andretti-Cadillac saga.", "source": {"uri": "sbnation.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "SB Nation"}, "authors": {"uri": "8150485697", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "20:44:30", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T20:44:30Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T20:43:44Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.4823529422283173, "url": "https://www.nytimes.com/athletic/3681665/2022/10/11/nascar-cole-custer-penalized/", "title": "NASCAR penalizes Cole Custer, No. 41 SHR team", "body": "NASCAR significantly penalized Cole Custer and his No. 41 Stewart-Haas Racing team Tuesday for attempting to ensure teammate Chase Briscoe advanced in the Cup Series playoffs by deliberately slowing to impede competitors during Sunday's Round 2 elimination race at the Charlotte Motor Speedway Roval.\n\nCuster was fined $100,000 and 50 driver points, SHR was docked 50 owner points, and crew chief Mike Shiplett was suspended indefinitely.\n\nCuster slowed considerably on the backstretch during the final lap of Sunday's race, as Briscoe, Austin Dillon and Erik Jones raced for position directly behind him. Upon Custer slowing down, Dillon then had to slow which allowed Briscoe to pass both drivers. NASCAR said Sunday after the race that the incident was under review and video, data and radio transmissions were to be analyzed to determine whether Custer had committed a rules violation.\n\nGreg Zipadelli, chief competition officer of Stewart-Haas, issued a statement on Wednesday.\n\n\"Stewart-Haas Racing denies any wrongdoing and will vigorously defend any personnel against these allegations in its appeal with NASCAR,\" he said.\n\nOn Tuesday, Scott Miller, NASCAR senior vice president of competition, said Shiplett was heard telling Custer, \"I think you got a flat (tire). Check up, check up,\" even though Shiplett could not see Custer's car.\n\n\"Obviously, pretty telling as to what went on there,\" Miller said. \"That, coupled with the data in the video and all the rest of the things that we looked into -- in-car videos and whatnot -- nothing contradicted the fact that was done deliberately by those individuals. So we were certainly forced to react.\n\n\"We can't have teams manipulating the finishing order.\"\n\nBriscoe was one of eight drivers who advanced out of the second playoff round Sunday; Kyle Larson, Daniel Suarez, Austin Cindric and Alex Bowman were the four drivers eliminated. Briscoe advanced to Round 3 by two points over Larson, the defending Cup champion.\n\nNo penalties were issued to Briscoe or his No. 14 SHR team. NASCAR determined Briscoe was already in position to transfer to the next round (via a tiebreaker) before Custer slowed and there were no radio transmissions from the team to Briscoe informing that Custer intended to run interference on his behalf.\n\nHad the evidence proved that Briscoe had knowledge of Custer's actions or had Briscoe moved into a transfer spot, Miller said NASCAR would've issued harsher penalties -- including the possible removal of Briscoe from the playoffs. In 2013, NASCAR removed Martin Truex Jr. from the playoffs after it was found that Michael Waltrip had conspired to manipulate the finish of the regular-season finale by directing Clint Bowyer to intentionally spinout to assist MWR teammate Truex in qualifying for the playoffs.\n\n\"(Briscoe) qualified for the Round of 8 without this, but had it not been the case and (Briscoe) would have gotten into the (next round) we would have had to react in an even stronger fashion,\" Miller said.\n\nA driver obstructing competitors to assist a teammate is not uncommon in NASCAR, with several recent examples -- including during the playoffs. Most notably, an incident between Hendrick Motorsports' Chase Elliott and SHR's Kevin Harvick that saw Elliott suffer a flat tire after contact with Harvick as they fought for position during a 2021 playoff race at Bristol Motor Speedway. After pitting for fresh tires, Elliott retaliated by returning to the track and deliberately slowing in front of Harvick, who was leading. Elliott's actions allowed Hendrick teammate Larson to pass Harvick for the lead and go on for the win.\n\nNASCAR did not penalize Elliott for impeding Harvick. Nor did the league penalize Chip Ganassi Racing's Ross Chastain when he intentionally blocked leader Kyle Busch so that CGR teammate Kurt Busch could pass his younger brother for the lead during the
